REPAYING MARKET STREET.
SINCE the Supervisors Have determined to re
pave Market street between Second and Sixth
with bituminous rock, it is to be hoped they
will introduce at least a few business principles into
the work. If as soon as the space between the "rib
bon"' already laid and the cable tracks is repaved it is
proposed to grant a franchise to another car company
to run down the highway, we respectfully suggest that
the francliise be granted and the track built prior to
the work now proposed being done.
We recall the fact that a few years ago Eddy street,
between Mason and Taylor, having risen to the dig
nity of a mudhole in winter and a sand pestilence in
summer, was paved with bituminous rock at an ex
. pense of $9000. The block was hardly dry ere the
Metropolitan Electric Company obtained a franchise
on Eddy street and proceeded to tear it up from end
to end. When the double track of this line had been
finished the beautiful pavement looked asÂ« though it
had been struck by a Kansas tornado. Substantially
the same thing happened on Stockton street between
Market and O"Farrell. The business men on this
.block suffered from unused car tracks, cobbles and
.dirt tor many years. Finally the city paved the block
with bitumen at an expense of about $5000. â  The
day after the work was done it occurred to the
Spring Valley Water Wojks that it needed a pipe in
the block, and the vandals of that corporation dug a
â  hole through it in short order.
If anything of this character needs to be done upon
Market street between Second and Sixth the time to
do it is before the pavement now projected by the
Supervisors is laid. At the very best the bitumen will
. be frequently torn up; it will be impossible to stay
the hands of the side sewer vandals and the building
â¢ operations of enterprising citizens. But the water,
â¢ gas and telephone companies can at least be com
pelled to put their pipes and conduits in order for a
long season of repose.
If something of this sort is not done, not only will
a great deal of money be wasted, but the street will
-.'â¢â¢ be maintained in a constant state of impassability.
Every time a bituminous pavement is torn up a per
: nianent scar is made which gradually develops into
â¢ a "chuck hole." As the city has no facilities for re
pairing "chuck holes," the first resort of the Street
Department is to fill them with macadam. This has
been done on Third street, which is now in a worse
condition than it was before the basalt blocks were
removed
If Market street is going to meet the fate that has
overtaken the streets we have named, it had better be
restored to the control of the street car monopoly
. arjd allowed to continue its revel with cobbles, ba
salt blocks, carbolized wood and asphalt. A great
improvement in the appearance and usefulness of the
magnificent thoroughfare can be made by introduc
ing into the work now in progress a little sense and
reason. The introduction of these things may shock
the politicians at the City Hall, but they ought to
stand it in the public interest.
ONE UNCONQUERABLE FOE.
AS to the necessity for hurrying Shafter's army
out of Cuba there can be no question. The
opinion among medical men is unanimous that
if . retained there the men must perish by thousands,
for the yellow fever cannot by any application of.
â¢science be much longer kept in abeyance. In case
of an outbreak it would find the troops weakened by
malaria and less deadly maladies, so that they would
be swept away. by thousands. There is no call for
such sacrifice.
One of the strongest statements of the conditions
prevailing there comes from "Colonel Roosevelt.
That he is a brave man he has proved. That he is a
patriotic and level-headed citizen was known before
Â° he led the Rough Riders on their splendid dash
toward Santiago. Yet Roosevelt is anxious to have
. the army moved to the north. He points out the
awful rcsutts of delay and the futility of condemning
soldiers to the devastation of the scourge. As he
makes clear, the men would not repine if by remain-
Ing at whatever risk they would accomplish aught
for their country. But for the time their work has
. been done, and done in a manner which was a glory
' to the land. Now let them be granted a haven of
: safety, a chance for life.
Even with peace seemingly assured the death list
has been long enough to carry sorrow to homes in
every State of the Union. There should not be
added to it uselessly another name.
. â¢ Just as Garcia is reported as having retired in dis
gust comes the news that he has met and defeated a
wing of the Sp nish forces. He appears to be about
â¢ as uncertain a quantity as several others which enter
into the composition of the situation.
â¢ The prospects that Americans may have to fight the
rebels in the Philippines ought to be more distressing
to the rebels than to anybody else.
A WOODPECKER CAMPAIGN.
POLITICAL contests begin much earlier in the
Eastern States than on this coast, and as a con
sequence the classification and naming of new
political features as they appear in each succeeding
campaign devolve upon the experts of that region.
In the contest that is now approaching the most
notable feature is the determination of the Demo
crats to make the fight on State issues only. This is
such a novelty in our politics that it requires a name
of its own to avoid confusion, and accordingly the
Eastern authorities on political nomenclature have
by general consent agreed to call it a "woodpecker"
campaign.
The name will be perplexing to some. It is but a
short time ago that Democracy was flitting about the
country as a cuckoo, answering to that call very
readily, and its appearance this year as a woodpecker
it must be admitted is somewhat surprising. In fact
there is no telling to what extent these rapid variety
changes may go. The old Democratic bird that be
gan its career as a game rooster may yet wind up as
a pewee.
Surprising as the name may be, however, it has not
been inaptly chosen, as any one may learn who
chooses to make a study of woodpeckers in relation
to Democrats. The Century Dictionary tells us
there are 250 species of woodpeckers, and it is well
known there are just that many kinds of Democratic
factions. If this be not sufficient proof that the two
creatures are birds of a feather, all doubts will be re
moved by the further statement of the dictionary
that the woodpecker is a scansorial, saurognathus,
sagittalingual pecarian; that the tongue is capable
of being thrust far out of the mouth and is lumbrici
form.
With these facts before us and with the campaign
rapidly approaching in our beloved State it is clearly
the duty of intelligent patriots to study woodpeckers.
Of the many species of the bird there are some of
special interest to us by reason of their numbers on
this coast and the part they are likely to play in the
contest. The most common of these is the Califor
nian woodpecker, Melanorpes formicivorus, de
scribed in the Encyclopedia Britannica as the most
provident bird in the woods, but withal so senseless
that he cannot distinguish an acorn from a pebble,
being about as apt to pick up the one as the other
on his foraging expeditions. This is the kind of a
woodpecker that in a political flurry would pick up
such a hard old Populist pebble as Maguire and
think it good stuff. '
Another local species deserving attention is the
hairy woodpecker, Picus dendrocopus villosus, a
rural creature of the Ah'ord type, hard to be dis
tinguished from a jay, and also incapable of knowing
a pebble from an acorn until after swallowing it.
Next come the yellow-bellied woodpeckers, com
monly known as sapsuckers, but recognized among
politicians as papsuckers. These yellow fellows are
exemplified in the McNab specimen and generally
pest with the Hearstlings.
We may pass the gilded woodpecker, a species of
gold bug votary who will not be conspicuous in the
campaign, but we cannot ignore the bristle-bellied
woodpecker of the genus asyndesmus. Many people
who have never heard of a woodpecker with bristles
will be inclined to doubt the existence of this species.
If such people, however, will only look around they
will perceive a good many of the bristling kind. As
a matter of fact, in some instances, as in that of the
Harney bird, the bristles just now are even more
conspicuous than the feathers, and the species is at
tested mainly by the sagittalingual tongue.
With such a variety of the game in sight, California
ought to make something like a picnic out of the
woodpecker campaign. It remains to be said only
that in this State there is no close season for wood
peckers. Anybody may take a pot shot at them at
any time.
FLINCHED UNDER FIRE.
WHILE there seem to be grounds for the belief
that the Seventy-first New York was tem
porarily panic-stricken before Santiago, the
men should not be too harshly condemned. As sol
diers they were entirely raw. The great majority
knew nothing more of war and its weapons than had
been learned in peaceful parades and picnic encamp
ments. They were facing a fearful fire coming from
an invisible foe. At first there were no trained troops
to set an example. These civilians, suddenly trans
formed into a fighting force, were at the front. If
for a time they gave way to the impulse of fear, they
seem to have redeemed themselves.
Any student of history knows that in battle there
are conditions under which even veterans quail. To
advance against a storm of bullets, not knowing the
strength of the enemy, not certain that there are re
inforcements at hand, is a terrible ordeal. Moreover,
a panic is an unaccountable phenomenon. Let it
once get started, whether from a tangible cause or a
mere prompting of timidity, and it spreads like a
flame in the dry grass. People who have not the
vaguest idea what they are scared about join in the
rout.
To such experiences as these soldiers are particu
larly subject. Cowardice is an infection, and one
faltering poltroon may spread dismay in every direc
tion.
WHAT GOES WITH THE FLAG.
IN a recent interview Mr. Hitt, one of the two Il
linois members of the Hawaiian Commission,
talked interestingly of the effect of our flag upon
the prosperity of the territory over which it floats.
He instanced General Grant's attempt to annex San
Domingo. During that effort Mr. Hitt visited San
Domingo and says that he found there representa
tives of American syndicates negotiating for fran
chises and privileges which covered the resources
and capacities of the country, but which were all taken
contingent upon annexation to the United States.
He transfers this condition to our possession of
Hawaii and, prospectively, to the countries and colo
nies we may take from Spain, and quotes it to show
what goes with our flag.
To a cool observer this seems to imply that the
transfer of the flag to more territory does not mean
an enlargement of the individual opportunities, but
opens a new field for the rapacity of combined cap
ital, made secure in its gains by franchises and privi
leges. There is no talk of an opening for individual
enterprise, of a field for hundreds of thousands of
American laborers in which to better their state and
advance more rapidly to independence. The lure is
to hundreds of millions of American dollars, with a
chance to increase their profits entrenched behind
privilege.
It must be confessed that this is not what the flag
always stood for. During more than a hundred years
it was an invitation to the man and not the dollar.
Men responded, and, flocking here from Northern
Europe, they brought thrift and industry, a fresh
stream to mingle with our original blood, and a de
votion to the principles of our government which
produced the enlarged opportunity for betterment of.
THE SAIN" FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 5, 1898.
the fortunes of the individual. Men feel emotions,
they thrill to patriotism, they bring to institutions
that enduring love of country which is necessary in
peace and war. To the accumulation of wealth there
is no objection, if it be not accompanied by the with
drawal of individual opportunity.
To say that special franchises and privileges go
with the flag is to emphasize a new era in our na
tional development, which may have to come in the
history of all nations, but that nation is the happiest
in which its coming is the longest delayed.
It has an evil sound when uttered in connection
with the annexation of Hawaii. The chief end of our
form of government has been supposed to be the
prosperity and happiness of the people brought under
its influence, and their encouragement by the full
protection given to the rights of person and prop
erty. But as man is the producer of wealth, by con
version thereinto of natural resources, his interests
as an individual can never be safely forgotten. That
they are in danger of being forgotten when wealth,
and not its producer, becomes the first object of
governmental care, seems to be true.
We fear that Mr. Hitt's idea of the advantages of
annexation and expansion sounds a note of alarm.
PROVOKED BY fl LIAR,
THERE are occasions which call for plain lan
guage and acts which must in honesty be de
nounced as knavish and cowardly. There are
liars who to th# sin of falsehood add the crime of in
gratitude and the despicable quality of cowardice.
Such an occasion was the appearance in the Kansas
City Times of an article, unsigned, traducing San
Francisco, and such an act was that of the nameless
and shameless poltroon who wrote it.
In this San Francisco is denounced as a modern
Sodom and Gomorrah, its citizens held up to ridicule,
and its ready and lavish hospitality maligned in such
terms as a blackguard may command.
There is an intimation throughout the missive that
it is the work of a member of the Kansas regiment.
If so that regiment, which by its deportment here has
made friends, about whose pitiful state of unprepared
ness upon arrival there was a storm of indignation,
whose incompetent Governor was denounced for
having sent his men unclad and unarmed, for whom
the Red Cross provided food and clothing, that regi
ment contains one man who is a sneak, who would
bite the hand extended to greet him. who would
thrust a knife into the back of a benefactor and who
in camp or in distant lands will be a disgrace to the
soldiers with whom he associates.
The letter enlarges upon the wickedness of the city.
All great centers of population have their evil spots.
San Francisco has its share. But the soldiers have
been warned concerning them. They have been
begged to stay away. The papers have solicited them
to avoid excesses; their officers have given them or
ders, but all in vain. In any city they would have had
a similar experience. The police have befriended
them, piloting them from dangerous quarters and re
fusing to arrest them unless offenses were too flag
rant to be overlooked. Perhaps the writer of the ar
ticle in question is suffering from the indiscretion of
not heeding well-meant counsel. Yet San Francisco
is accustomed to the libel of being called wicked, and
will pass this by. One street is described as being
like a lane through Hades. While the accuracy of
the description must remain in doubt, common sense
would suggest that a street of this character be
avoided.
But there are specific libels. "California regiments
then stationed here were made up of the worst class
of south of Market hoodlums," continues this measure
less liar, and he tells about the "sallow-faced" Cali
fornians who were raised with a "hop can" and
"fairy lamp" at their side. He adds that there is no
law here against opium-smoking. While the futility
of replying to one who is so completely vicious, so
absolutely dead to the sense of decency, in fact such
a fool, is apparent so far as touching his intellectual
Â»*oid and his absent conscience is concerned, there
are people at a distance whom he might mislead.
The Californians who have gone to the front consti
tute a regiment made up of the best blood of the
West, fine, manly, patriotic, a thousand strong; they
went with the tears and blessings of people who
loved them and took pride in them. No better regi
ment serves under the flag we honor. But this mis
erable scoundrel must traduce them, slander them, try
to give out the impression that these big, brave boys
are opium fiends! Was there ever affront more con
temptible? The fellow deserves to be whipped from
the army as a snarling and rabid cur.
His crowning insult is to the hospital^ which has
been lavished on every soldier. He says it was all
for the Native Sons of the Golden West. The whelp
lies again knowingly and maliciously. It cannot have
escaped him that in jhis hospitality there has been
no distinction. The First California Regiment was
early removed from the scene. Then came other
regiments, from lowa, Nebraska, Minnesota, Penn
sylvania, Kansas, Montana, the Dakotas, Utah, Ore
gon, Washington, Tennessee, Wyoming; at inter
vals there would be additions to the regular army.
All of these were greeted with equal fervor, all made
welcome, all fed. They were all "our boys" and the
common heart went out to them. Some were poorly
equipped, ragged, unarmed. For such there was a
particular manifestation of regard. Money was poured
out freely. Women gave their time to providing
necessities which the Government had been slow to
furnish. Thousands of dollars were freely contrib
uted and spent for blankets, shoes, delicacies. The
badge of the Red Cross became a badge of honor,
and none was too proud to wear it. There had been
official neglect, or at least tardiness, and every effort
was made to remedy this. For the hardships the boys
suffered at the hands of unscrupulous contractors the
greatest indignation was among the people of San
Francisco, and the outcry they raised was the means
of bringing the rascals to time. When a camp site
was selected where the cold winds brought in the
fogs it was the people who protested and caused re
moval to a more healthful spot. From all over the
country came messages of gratitude for the atten
tions bestowed on brothers and sons.
This Kansas mongrel struck the first discordant
note. Does he know that a humble employe of this
paper, happening to hail from Kansas, started a sub
scription among fellow workmen and presented, to
the Kansas regiment an expensive set of surgical
instruments? Or, knowing it, is he a cur too yellow
to appreciate? The gift was never acknowledged,
but no matter. When the men of the regiment learn
of it they will be grateful. It is not the stray, yelping
canine from which thanks are to be expected.
San Francisco is glad to have the Kansas regiment
here. It has the fullest sympathy with the ambition,
to sail for the field of action. When the regiment de
parts it will be given a farewell and a hearty god
speed, for with all our faults we are not so small as
to judge the men of Kansas, the loyal, young citi
zens, by the one debased and scurrilous scoundrel
who has invaded their ranks.
It is as we suspected. When Aguinaldo's head had
a $25,000 price set upon it it began to swell.

LIEUTTNANT
TO COLONEL
IN A DAY.
"Just after we left Ogden a sergeant
In one of the companies came up to me
and with a more than ordinary respect
ful salute begged me to allow him to get
off at the next station and fill his can
teen, as he was feeling ill and needed
fortifying of both spirit and the body.
He seemed to be the personification of
honesty, and I granted him permission
to do as he deaired.
"The next station was passed and he
appeared again. "Well, sergeant, what is
wrong now?' I asked. 'If you please,
captain, I am a very sick man, and I
need some more medicine,' he replied.
Of course, I told him that at the next
station he might refill his canteen, but
advised him not to take too much.
Hardly had I settled myself for a com
fortable sleep on the seats, after passing
the- second stop on the route, when Mr.
Sergeant appears again. This time it was:
'Major, 1 am feeling worse and worse;
wouldn't you please let me till this tin
again at the next station?' Hoping that
the next canteen would prove disabling,
I told him It was all right. The next
station was reached and passed, and
there stood the gentleman with bibulous in
clinations, pleading just one more favor.
'Now, colonel, I need ' this medicine
badly. Let me off at the next sta-shun
anil be your fren fer- llfâ sure.'
"I had to htop his medicine taking for
my own protection. If I had allowed that
man to continue lon the same tack I
would have been on the retired list long
before I reached San Francisco."

HISTOBIC VALLEY FOBGE.
To many of those who celebrated Evac
uation day at Valley Forge the visit was
a revelation. For the first time they re
alized the extent of that historic winter
encampment and saw for themselves that
what has been acquired by the State for
the purpose of restoration is but a small
portion of the territory occupied by
"Washington and his troops during the
memorable winter and that if any proper
restoration is to take place the remain-
Ing territory covered by the encampment
must be acquired. Some half-fllled earth
works still exist and the remnants of a
system of defensive fortifications are
still visited, but very little remains to fur
nish e\fc?n an outline of the Valley Forge
encampment in its entirety. With the
maps and descriptions still available a
restoration could be effected, and the
sooner the land Is acquired and the work
undertaken the better. AVhile no battle
was fought at Valley Forge, it was In
this historic camp that the revolutionary
soldiers were transformed by discipline I
from raw. untrained militia to the well
drilled, heroic army that compelled the I
evacuation of a score of field's in the
South and triumphed grandly at York
town. Schooled in the hardships and pri
vation of winter camp these heroic men I
here acquired the patience, discipline, en- |
durance and unconquerable love of liber- j
ty that made them invincible on the fu
ture battlefields of the revolution.âPhil
adelphia Times.

locations where hydrographic surveys
have been mafle.

AN ALTEN MlNOÃâ Subscriber, Peta
luma. Cal. An alien under the age of 21
years who has resided in the United
States three years next preceding his ar
riving at that age, and who has continued
to reside therein to the time he may make
application to be admitted a citizen there
of, may. after he arrives at the age of 21
years, and after he has resided five years
within the United States, including the
three years of his minority, be admitted
a citizen; but he must make a declaration
on oath and prove to the satisfaction of
the court that for two years next preced
ing it was his bona fide intention to be
come a citizen. If you came here at the
age of 16 and come within the provisions
quoted you can become a citizen without
taking out first papers.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 5, 1898.
FURLOUGHS FOR THE SICK.
Modified Order Transmitted by wire From
Washington to General Merriam.
ADJUTANT GENERAL CORBIN'S order granting thirty days' fur
lough to convalescent soldiers has, of necessity, been greatly restrict
ed. The original order gave power to grant furloughs to regimental
and detachment surgeons and commanders, and it was apparent to
every one that one of the largest effects of the order would be a big in
crease in the sick list.
With this result in view, further instructions were asked of General
Merriam. and yesterday he received a telegram from Washington, which
is incorporated in the following circular:
HEADQUARTERS INDEPENDENT DIVISION, EIGHTH ARMY CORPS, CAMP
MERRITT, SAN FRANCISCO, Cal., August 4, 1898.
The telegram from the Adjutant General. U. 8. A., of the Ist inst., relative to
granting lurloughs by surgeons in charge of hospitals. Is limited in its application
to the surgeon In charge of the Division Field Hospital.
Sick in regimental or other hospitals to whom furloughs should be gTanted
should be transferred to Division Field Hospital.
In all cases descriptive lists of the men to whom furloughs are granted will be
sent to these headquarters.
By command* of Brigadier General Miller.
CHARLES G. WOODWARD.
First Lieutenant Third Artillery, Acting- Adjutant General.
gage of the soldiers and engineer* was
got aboard the transports yesterday
afternoon. The engineers' camp will be
entirely obliterated, while only seven
companies will be left in the New York
Regiment, and unless these are sent on
either the Arizona or Scandia the*' are
likely to remain here for some time.
Although no orders were issued last
night, it was stated, upon excellent au
thority, that the transports will sail this
afternoon. This report ie in a measure
confirmed by a statement from the Post
office authorities, made last night, to the
effect that mail for Honolulu by the
steamer Charles Nelson would close this
morning at 8:30 o'clock.
DRILL OF THE KANSANS.
Attracts a Largo Attendance to
the Army and Navy Chris
tian Commission Benefit.
The men of the Twentieth Kansas Regi
ment. United States Volunteers, under
command of Colonel Frederick Funston.
drilled last night in Mechanics' Pavilion
under the auspices of the Army and Navy
The men worked like veterans and won
the highest commendation from General
Marcus P. Miller, the reviewing officer.
The spectators were, provided with flags
â¢which they waved upon every possible
occasion.
The tid-bit of the programme was sim
ply announced: Company drill, by Com
pany X, Captain Edmund Boltwood. The
drill proved an elaborate affair. In ad
dition to regular evolutions the men gave
an excellent exhibition of fancy drilling.
Their work throughout was punctuated
with applause.
Guard mount was performed in double
quick time, an innovation here. The offi
cers were: Adjutant. Captain Fred E.
Buthan; old officer of the day. Captain
Henry B. Orwlg; new officer of the day,
Captain Charles I. Martin: officer of
the guard,, .Lieutenant Huddlc-fton; ser
geant major. Frederick R. Dodge.
Mme. Alice Waltz sang the "Star
Spangled Banner" to the accompaniment
of the regimental band. The band added
much to the beauty of the drills through
out the evening.
The committee on reception of invited
guests was composed of:
R. H. Chamberlain, Rev. A. T. Need
ham, B. C. Wright, Key. Charles Edward

been rilled by Dr. Huffman of the Twen
tieth Kansas.

of men discharged will be much greater
than wan first estimated. Tho first es

to the company; later developments Bhow
an increase to seven or eight to the com-

killed DanlelF.
With the rest of the men of hiÃ com

ILL AT CAMP BARRETT.
First Case 01 o^rious Sickness
Reported in tho Eighth
California.
CAMP BARRETT. Bather Tract, Aug.
.4.â The first case of what Is regarded as
serious. sickness " was reported to-day at
the headquarters of the hospital corps,
and the patient, Private Frank Bush, of
Company F of Oakland, was immediately
removed to Fabioia Hospital. He was
first taken, sick a week or so ago, suffer
ing from a severe cold. The last four
days, however, his temperature ran ex
ceedingly high, and strong symptoms of
typhoid-pneumonia â¢ were manifested to
day; hence his removal.
The quartermaster's department was
kept busy to-day on account of the ar
rival of a large shipment of shoes, uni
forms, blouses and trousers, blanket bogs,
for marching and complete equipment of
shelter tents for the entire regiment.
Colonel Henshaw has ordered all supplies
.to be equally distributed among the vari
ous companies. The regiment is being
conducted on as. practically a military
basis as possible. The proposition is be
ing reached of having the regiment look
first-class and yet carry no surplus. The
regiment '* is : now equipped with every
practical necessity, and is in a position
to be ordered to the front at any moment.
Much has been accomplished in a short
space of time, although the Eighth has
not yet been attached to any brigade.

PREFERRED
DEATH TO
SEPARATION
Mrs. Van Ness Tries
to End Her Life.
VICTIM OF DISCARDED LOVE
HANDSOME LIEUTENANT WHO
PROVED ADAMANT.
Dr. Rodgers Removes the Bullet and
Gives Hope for Her Hecovery. .
Strict Secrecy Was
Enjoined.
A pistol shot startled the inmates of a
lodging-house on Post street, near Grant
avenue, last night. The smell of powder
issued from an open transom and the
braver inmates of the house forced open
the door and found a handsome young
woman, a Mrs. Van Ness, stretched out
on the carpet with a revolver at her
side.
A messenger was hastily dispatched for
a physician, and with the moans and in
articulate cries for the man she loved the
attempted suicide lapsed into uncon
sciousness.
On the arrival of Doctor Rodgers she
revived and pointed to an ugly wound in
her arm. The physician administered an
anesthetic, and after a. great deal of
probing dislodged a thirty-two caliber
bullet.
When Mrs. Van Xess recovered con
sciousness she continued to moan and
bee to be allowed to die. The perfidy of
a heartless man was the reason for her
act. She would first pray to see the man
*he loved and then hurl anathemas upon
his head for forsaking her.
Every effort was made to hush up the
affair by the friends of the hysterical
woman. It appears that Mrs. Van NfiU,
who is a very intelligent and well trav
eled woman, arrived in this city about
a month ago, bringing 1 with her trunks
filled with the fruits of her extensive
travel. She took the finest apartment In
the apartment hou^e and engaging a
colored maid set up housekeeping. Her
rooms were decorated with beautiful Jap
anese and Indian curios and furnished
in a manner befitting a woman of re
fined taste and one used to luxury. She
dressed in the height of fashion and at
tracted great attention by her appear
ance. Among her few visitors was a
handsome young lieutenant who arrived
from the Eaut a short time ago with his
regiment, which is now encamped at the
Presidio. He Wftfl often ycon in her com
pany and everything pointed to tho fact
that a b'>nd of afffx-tion existed between
them. She often accomiianled him to
dinner and places of amusement until a
slight quarrel estranged them. His visits
ceased entirely and the charming woman
began to show signs of his absence. She
no longer dressed in bright colors and
seldom left her apartments. Last night
she learned that he wan about to leave
for Manila on one of the outgoing trans
ports, and feeling that she had nothing
more to live for tried to end her exist
ence.
She sent her maid out on a frivolous
errand and. taking a pearl-handled re
volver from her bureau, pointer! it at her-
Mlf and fired. Luckily she was nervous
and left-handed, and the hall passed
through her right breast and lodg-ed in
her arm.
Although the wound If painful Dr. Rod
prrs believes she will recover.
As to the lieutenant who caused the

A SERIOUS CHARGE.
B. J. Mitchell, a cousin to Colonel Fun
ston, has been detailed as acting quar
termaster sergeant of the Twentieth
Kansas, instead of James Young, now
under arrest for embezzlement. Young
had his first hearing before the Second
Brigade court martial yesterday. Court
adjourned until Monday without having
heard all of the evidence in the case.
War Incidents.
General Miller and his staff have ac
cepted an invitation to be present at the
farewell reception to be given on Satur
day evening to the Seventh California by
the Red Cross Society.
Leave of absence for fifteen days has
been granted First Lieutenant Thomas
F. Barry of the First Battalion, Heavy
Artillery, California Volunteers.
Artificer Arthur M. Pino, Company G,
First Infantry, has been granted a fur
lough for three months.
Private Edward C. Shearman. Battery
L, Third Artillery, has been sentenced to
three months' imprisonment at hard la
bor and to forfeit $30 of his pay, for using
disrespectful, obscene and insubordinate
language to a non-commissioned officer.
Leave of absence for fifteen days has
been granted to Major Edward B. Mose
ley, surgeon.
Sergeant Pugh, Company D, First In
fiintry, has been granted a furlough for
four months, to take effect upon his re
enlistment.
Private Peter Petersen, Company H,
First Infantry, has been sentenced to be
dishonorably discharged from the service
of the United States, to forfeit all pay
due him, and to be imprisoned at hard
labor for six months, for absence without
leave, disobedience of the orders of a non
commissioned officer, and desertion.
Private Dunlay, Light Battery C, Third
Artillery, for drunkenness and using
threatening and insubordinate language
to a non-commissioned officer, has been
sentenced to be dishonorably discharged
from the service, to forfeit all pay and to
be imprisoned for three months at hard
labor.
Private William La Vere, Battery D,
Third Artillery, guilty of attempting to
suppress evidence upon a charge of lar
ceny, has been sentenced to be discharged
from the service, to forfeit all pay, and
to be imprisoned for three months at hard
labor.
Private Samuel Breeding, Battery D,
Third Artillery, guilty of larceny, has
been sentenced to be dishonorably dis
charged from the service, forfeiting all
pay, and to be imprisoned for one year
at hard labor.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver unchanged.
' Wheat dull and nominal.
Barley higher again.
/Oats, Corn and' Rye unchanged.
Receipts of Hay continue to decrease. '
' White Beans Mill firm.
, Potatoes and .Onions about the same.
- Tomatoes firmer and Green Cora weaker.
Butter advanced.
No change in. Eggs and Cheese.
Poultry market quiet. ! ,
''Bartlett Pears weaker.
Peaches and Apricots firm.
Prunes quoted rather firmer. J
Nothing new in Provisions.
Merchandise unaltered.
Shipments of $83,973 to China.
REVENUE COLLECTIONS.
Internal Revenue collections In this district
-In July already showed the natural increase
due to the operation of the new tax law, and
amounted to ?R5LÂ»,244, against $446,693 for the
.same month in 1897. For the first seven
months of the year -the collections were $1.
657 &42. against $1,7>15.59". in 1897, and included
$455,166 for beer, U 16.017 for spirits and $124,561
"for cigars and tobacco.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Aug. 4.â was obvious from
the developments in to-day's stock market that
wealthy and powerful speculators were plac
ing very large amounts of money in the granger
stocks in anticipation of a rise in prices. There
' were many indications also that some of the
funds which were being placed in the grangers
were delved from sales of some of the more
prominent Industrial specialties, which have
shown such recent large advances In price as
- to yield a handsome profit on the sale. ' This
shifting of speculation is an episode which
invariably occurs In the course of a long-con
tinued advance in prices. The preliminary
movement- in the highly speculative industrials
is generally quite apart from any general con
dition of business and Industry. The late ;
awakening of the more substantial railroad
stocks is looked upon as Indicating a more vital
stage of speculation -and investment. There
was a broad and confident 'â¢ demand to-day
. through the whole of the .railroad list, which
most have -come from the outside public. In
â  many stocks .this growing demand was met by
a steady stream of offerings which sent prices.
In spite of quite a large Volume of transactions,
only slightly higher than last night. The shift-
Ing of speculative accounts- involving profit
taking in the industrials encouraged bear at
tacks, but the whole railway list held remark
ably firm, showing a strong, tone at or near
the top. â  "THiIM - '
There were very heavy dealings in railroad
bonds to-day, the demand being very widely
distributed,- but most urgent in the reorganiza
, tion 4 per 'cents. The total sales of $7,475(000 is
eaid to be the largest day's business on record.
There were large dealings in the Government
Ss to-day at 106, one block of (415,000 going at
that price. A small block of the issue sold for
105%. United States old 4s coupon advanced
% in the bid price. There was no other change.
â¢ The total sales of stocks to-day were 320,800
shares, including-: Atchiaon preferred, 7634;
Burlington. 33,532; Louisville and Nashville,
6920; Metropolitan, 3265; Missouri Pacific, 8390:
Northern Pacific, 5615; Northern Pacific pre
ferred, 10,040; Rock Island, 23,098: St. Paul,
39,374: Southern preferred, 3536; Union Pacific
preferred, 12,220; Union .Pacific, Denver and
Gulf, 8573; Cotton Oil. 9450; Tobacco, 15, 265;
People's Gas, 10.900; Sugar, 33,145; Leather pre
ferred, 3040; Rubber, 9167; Western Union, 6461.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, Aug. 4.â The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says: The stock
markets were quiet to-day, but still good, with
a further rise in Spanish securities a feature.
There is a good demand for Americans, espe
cially from New Turk. Indeed. New York buy
ing Of bonds and shares is bo large that it is
believed that it will check gold exports to the
States. Prices closed practically at the best.
Grand Trunks were good.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO. Aug. 4.â Weak Liverpool cables
.and generally flne weather throughout the
United Kingdom and Continent started wheat
lower. Some phases of the domestic situation.
however, were anything but favorable to thÂ«
bears. As the bullish factors began Imme- I
diately after the opening to be emphasized by
figures the market quickly threw off the im
pression with which it started. Chicago re
ceived only TO cars against 149 the correspond
ing day last year. Minneapolis and Duluth
receipts were 77 carloads against 150 the pre
vious year. In consequence the shipping de
mand for wheat was extremely good. The
threshing returns from Ohio were said to have
forced a reduction In the estimated yield in |
that State of fully 60 per cent from a month I
ago. That was also helpful to the bulls. The i
close was %@%c higher for September. Decem- I
tier is unchanged. i"~: :
The first bull inspiration corn has had In ;
several days was the statement to-day by the
Price Current claiming that only three quarters
of a crop would be raised. This seemed to
brace the nerves of some longs who needed en
couragement to keep them from selling out.
The general tone of the market became strong
and continued so to the end. September closed
with a gain of %<s%c. -. â¢<
Harvesting returns, which showed an In
creasing deficiency in the crop, together with
the strength of the other grains, advanced oats.
September closed %c higher.
Provisions ruled firm on light speculative
offerings and a good trade in the cash products.
The rally In corn was also an Influence. Pork
left oft 5c higher, lard 10c and ribs ."c higher.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic
Office located in the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
light*, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
luil Ming, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon. 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
in the morning papers the following day.
CHA9. P. WELCH.
Ensign (retired), U. S. N.. In charge.

are strong Pardee towns, and in both
places' Harris baa provided every facility
for voters to cast their ballots. Polling
places have been located at frequent in
tervals; thus enabling the Pardee con
stituency to get in their votes with little
inconvenience or loss of time. ' â¢
On the other hand, Sunol and Altamont

location would seem to indicate that they
were selected- for no other purpose than
to discourage voters from Hunting them
out. In one Instance the nearest polling
place was located at a point that would
require a considerable number of voters
living in one neighborhood (and all Davis
men) to make a round trip of several
miles in order to vote.
At Tcsla, the new coal mining camp,
the situation is even worse, and so far
Harris has manifested no disposition to
correct it. In order to vote, the miners,
who. are almost to a man for Davis,
would have to lose a day's work and
travel to Liivermore, nearly ten miles dis
tant. The superintendent positively re
fuses to grant the miners leave of ab
sence, as- it would necessitate the closing
down of the mine.
Consequently" there are a large number
of Davis votes in Tesla that will not be
cast unless Harris can be prevailed upon
to "provide a polling place, in that town.
The protests in some localities have be
come so pronounced that Committeeman
Harris has been forced to take some
steps -toward pacifying the complainants
by providing .more polling places and
changing others to more central loca
tions, but even the present arrangement
is far from satisfactory in those places.
It. Is by such frauds and with the aid
of the Buckley-Magnlre Democratic out
laws that Dr. Pardee expects to carry
the county. That his case is desperate
is amply demonstrated by his resorting
to such underhand methods. â

The. straight Populist County Commit
tee met to-night and adopted a resolution

gates to the late State convention in nom
inating Maguire for Governor, declaring

spirit of tin- State Central Committee In
calling the convention and commending
those delegates who held a separate con

ulist State ticket, calling upon all Ala
meda County Populists to support such
ticket.
Resolutions adopted by the Lorln and
the Forty-ninth Assembly District Popu
list Clubs indorsing the course of the
county committee in withdrawing from
the fusion wing were presented. The
members discussed plans for carrying on
the campaign for the ticket headed by

canvass of Alameda County.

MALOON'S MEMORIES
OF EARLY OAKLAND
MANY STREETS WERE OPEN
TO THE WATER FRONT.
â 
Slow Progress in the Trial That May
Permanently Settle Four Long
Years of Litiga
tion. â¢
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Aug. 4.
The feature of to-day's hearing in the
I Oakland water front suit was a series of
! contentions between opposing counsel as
| to the method of proving the dedication
by the city of Oakland of property along
the front between the low and high tide
j lines for the use of the public at the time
of the compromise of 1888.
Attorney Pringle, conducting the case
| for the city, contended the proper way to
be by testimony of users of the streets.
He proposed to introduce a number of
old residents to establish this point. A
considerable controversy ensued.
Henry Maloon, who has resided in Oak
land from his infancy, and who in his
testimony showed a remarkable memory
and acquaintance with streets as they
existed prior to 1868, was the principal
' witness of the day. When asked whether
| or not prior to ivtis thore was any city
here having streets and houses Attorney
Moore registered an emphatic objection
i on the ground of Incompi tency tor the
reason thai before oral testimony may
I be introduced to- prove the dedication evi
dence must be produced to show that the
city or the owners made an offer of
dedication over and across land which
was own d by the person making the
offer of dedication, so that by user or the
like dedication could be made complete.
Attorney .Moore contended that if there
was any attempt to show dedication over
lands between Ordinary high and low
ti.le lines to connect with Bileola laid
down on the Kellersberger map it would
first be necessary that it should appear
by competent evidence- that the city, in
passing the ordinance shown in the rec
ords already in evidence, owned or had
control over the land alleged to have be
come dedicated.
After considerable argument by oppos
ing counsel Judge Ogden finally overruled
Attorney Moore's objections and permit
ted proof by user.
Witness Maloon occupied the stand the
entire afternoon and his testimony was
interesting as showing the condition of
the streets prior to the (Seeding of the
property to Carpentier by the city.
Maloon held his own all through the
cross-examination by Moore. lie de
scribed in detail the condition of streets
west of Market street as they existed
between 18C3 and ixtX; the rapidity with
Which that section was populated and im
proved; he admitted that none of the
streets In the district mentioned were in
an improved condition at that time, but
showed clearly that many ran out a con
siderable distance in 1863 to 186S, when
they were used by oyster men In hauling
oysters from the schooners to high land.
On account of three of the attorneys
being engaged in the Supreme Court the
n.xt few days the case went over until
next Tuesday afternoon.

tic Speech to the Unitarian Club.
ALAMEDA, Auk. 4.â At last night's BeastOÃ
of the Vnltarlan t'lub John P. Irish delivered
a speech on the pubject of. "The Proposed Co
lonial Imperial Policy of Our Government."
He took strong ground against the ftcquisltli D
of any foreScu territory by this country and
declared that it was against the constitution.
If we persisted in adopting such a policy we
should drop the Fourth of July and take the

Should some home, pay our own bills and drop
it. After the address was finished there \v;ih
a good deal of subdued comment among those
of the members who did not approve of Irish's

Death of J. li. Browne.
OAKLAND, Aug. 4.â J. L. Browne, a prom
inent Odd Follow and old resident of this
city, died yesterday at Los Ottos. Deceased
was cashier of the Oakyand Rank of Savings
from IS6S to 1572, but had not been actively en
gaged of late years. He was a charter member
of Golden Rule Encampment No. 34, I. O. O.
F., past grand representative to the Grand
Lodge, past grand of Oakland Lodge No. 118,
and was also a member of the Pioneer Odd
Fellows cf the coast. lie was a native of
New York, and 70 years of age. The funeral
will take place to-morrow . afternoon at 2
o'clock.

SENSATIONAL TRIAL OPENS
COL. EVANS RETUBNED ''TOBAC
CO SMELLING TRASH."
The Plaintiff Makes a Deposition and
Describes the Papers She
la Anxious to
Regain.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
9UB Broadway, Aug. 4.
The first stop in the trial of the suit
brought by Mrs. Pedar Sather against
Colonel .T. V. Evans. Collector of Customs
for Manila, was taken to-day, when
Mrs. Batter testified before the Commis
sioner. The deposition was taken by At
torneys M. M. Kstee, Harold Martin and
W. J. Robinson to the latter's office !n
this city before NotaJ-y R. J. Montgom
ery. BCassrs. A. A. Moore and John A.
Sanborn were pres -nt to represent Mrs.
Sather. The deposition was filed with the
court this afternoon.
The purpose of the deposition was to
obtain from Mrs. Sather detailed infor
mation regarding the one hundred and
ten documents for which she is suing.
When the suit was filed two weeks ago
Mrs. Sather declined to say one word re
garding it, and her deposition is the first
testimony given by the plaintiff.
Mrs. Sather related that some time ago
Colonel Evans was her attorney and that
be had full charge of her affairs. He In
vestigated the business transactions be
tween Mrs. Sather and William J. Dingee,
who had been her agent. It was discov
ered that there was about JlS.ow) differ
ence in favor of Mrs. Sather and at this
lime M. M. Estee was consulted, 10-dtiy
Mrs. Bather said that it was with Mr.
Kst.e s knowledge that she paid the in
terest on the mortgage represented by
the (18,000, ii ml she also understood that
he wits aware that she had agreed to pay
tin: principal. , _, ,
Mrs. Sather stated that when Colonel
Evans acted as her attorney she turned
over to him all the papers relating to her
transactions with Mr. Dingee. \\ hen the
colonel settled with Mrs. Sather she says
he did not return the valuable documents,
but "a lot of old trash, smelling very
strongly of tobacco."
On being pressed to accurately describe
the documents, Attorney Moore objected
on the ground that Mrs. Sather had al
ready stated that they were financial
statements between her and her agents.
The witness state.) that the papers she
sought were monthly statements relat
ing to the collection of rents and the pur
chase and sale of real estate. She believed
there were one hundred and ten of these
statements not in her possession, and she
further believes that they would be of
great value to her should she be able to
procure them.
At one time when Colonel Evans called
on her in relation to disputed matters,
Mrs. Sather testified, he said, "My dear
child, why did you do that?"
â¢Who called you 'my dear child'?"
ejaculated Mr Estee, creating the lmpres- I
sion that he thought she was testifying
of her conversation with him.
"Colonel Evans," said the lady, where
upon Mr. Estee appeared to be much re
lieved.
On the whole Mrs. Sather's evidence
was not particularly satisfactory to the
d< Cendant, as the attorneys did not elicit
from her the detailed nature of the docu
ments which they desired.
Attorney Sanborn is trying to got the
papers t>i enable a complete examination
to be made into th* affairs of Mrs. Sather.
About a year ago Mr. Dingee ceased to
be the ngent of Mrs. Safher, and her
new agents. Heron & Holoomb, put an ex
pert to work on her former accounts. A
little discrepancy was found and she then
employed Colonel Evans as her attorney.
Dealings between them were not altogeth
er satisfactory, and Mr. Sanborn became :
her attorney and brought suit to recover |
the missing monthly statements. Mr. i
I>ing.-e says if any of the statements
have been lost they can easily be dupli
cated. He declares he does not under- ;
stand how Mrs. Sather can place any
value such as $25,000 on the missing pa
pers, and he says that he has Mrs. Sath
er's acknowledgment that all accounts be
tween them are settled and correct.

PROCLAMATION.
STATE OF CALIFORNIA.
EXECUTIVE DEPARTMENT,
SACRAMENTO, July 3<>th. IG9B.
WHEREAS, The Legislature of the State of
California, at its thirty-second' session begin-
ning on the fourth day of January, A. D. 1597,
two-thirds of all the members elected to each
of the two houses of said Legislature voting
in favor thereof, proposed the following de-
scribed amendments to the Constitution of the
State of California, to-wit:
AMENDMENT NUMBER ONE.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
41.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California, an amendment to section
eighteen of article eleven of the Constitu-
tion, in relation to revenue and taxation, by
which it is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows: . .
Section IS. No county, city, town, township,
board of education, or school district shall in-
cur any indebtedness or liability in. any man-
ner or for any purpose exceeding in any year
the income and revenue provided for It Tor
such year, without the assent of two-thirds
of the qualified electors thereof, voting at an
election to be held for that purpose, nor un-
less before, or at the time of incurring such
Indebtedness, provision shall be made tor the
collection of an annual tax sufficient to pay
the Interest on such Indebtedness as It falls
due, and also provision to constitute a sinking
fund for the payment of the principal thereof
on or before maturity, which shall not exceed
forty years from the time of contracting the
same; provided, however, that the City and
Count; of San Francisco may at any time pay
the unpaid claims with interest thereon for
materials furnished to and work done for saia
city and county during the forty-third ana
forty-fourth fiscal years, out of the income and
revenue, of any succeeding year or years; pro-
vided, that any and all claims for making, re-
pairing, altering or for any work done upon or
for any material furnished for any street, lane,
alley, court, place or sidewalk, or for the con-
struction of any sewer or sewers In said city
and county are hereby excepted from the pro-
visions of this section: and in determining any
claim permitted to be paid by this section, no
Ftntute of limitations shall apply in any man-
ner; and provided further, that the City of
Vallejo, in Solano County, may pay Its exist-
ing Indebtedness incurred in the construction
of its waterworks, whenever two-thirds of the
electors thereof voting at on election held for
that purpose, shall so decide. Any indebted-
ness or liability incurred contrary to this pro-
vision, with the exception hereinbefore recited,
shall be void.
AMENDMENT NUMBER TWO.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
10.)
A resolution proposing to the people of the
State of California an amendment to the Con-
stitution of tlie State, by adding a new sec-
tion, to be known and designated as section
seven and one-half, article eleven thereof, pro-
viding for the framing by the inhabitants of
counties, of local county government acts for
their own government. The said proposed new
section to read as follows:
Section IVt- The inhabitants of any county
may frame a county government act for their
own government, relating to the matters here-
inafter specified, and consistent with, and sub-
ject to, the Constitution and laws of this btate.
by causing a board of fifteen freeholders, who
have beer* for at least five years, qualified
electors of such county, to be elected by the
qualified electors of such county, at any gen-
eral or special election, whose duty it snail be
within ninety days after such election, to pre-
pare and propose a county government act for
such county, which shall be signed in dupli-
cate by the members of such board, or a ma-
jority of them, and returned, one copy thereof
to the Board of Supervisors or other legisla-
tive body of such county, and the other copy
to be sent to the Recorder of Deeds of the
county Such proposed county government act
shall then be published in two papers of gen-
â¢ eral circulation In such county, or if there be
. not two such papers, then In one only, for at
least twenty ('ays, and within not less than
i thirty days after such publication it shall be
submitted' to the qualified electors of such
county, at a general or special election, and If
1 a majority of such qualified electors voting
thereon shall ratify the same, it shall there-
after be submitted to the legislature for Its
rejection or approval, as a whole, without
power of alteration or amendment, and if ap-
proved by a majority of the members elected
to each house. It shall be the county govern-
ment act of/ such county, and shall In such
case become the organic law thereof and super-
sede any existing county government act. and
all amendments thereof, and all special laws
inconsistent with such county government act.
A copy of su.-h county government act, certi-
f., fl tiy the President of the Board of Super-
visors or other legislative body of such county,
and authenticated by the eeal of such county,
setting forth the submission of such county
government act to the electors, and its ratifica-
tion by them, shall be made in duplicate and
deposited, one in the office of the Secretary of
State the other, after being recorded In the
office of the recorder of deeds in the county,
among the archives of the county.
All courts shall take judicial notice thereof.
The county government act so ratified may be
amended at Intervals of not less than two
years by proposals therefor, euhmltted by the
legislative authority of the county, to the
qualified electors thereof, at a general or
special election held at least forty days after
the publication of such proposals for twenty
days In a newspaper of general circulation in
such county, and ratified by at least three-
fifths of the qualified electors voting thereon,
and approved by the Legislature as herein pro-
vided for the approval of the county govern-
ment act In submitting any such county gov-
ernment act any alternative article or proposi-
tion may be presented for the choice of the
voters, and may be voted on separately with-
out prejudice to other?.
It shaii be competent In all county govern-
ment acts framed under the authority given by
thN section, to provide for the manner In
which the times at which, and the terms for
which' the several township and county officers
other than Judges of the Superior Court, shall
be elected or appointed; for their compensa-
tion- for the number of such officers, for the
consolidation or segregation of offices, for the
number of deputies that each officer shall have,
and for the compensation payable to each of
such deputies, for the manner In which, the
times in which, and the terms for which the
i members of all boards of election shall be
elected or appointed: and for the constitution,
regulation, compensation and government of
Â«uch boards, ar.d of their clerks and attaches;
also to prescribe the manner and method by
which all elections by the people shall be con-
ducted: and may in addition determine the
tests and conditions upon which electors, po-
litical parties ami orp.inizatlons may partici-
pate in any primary election.
Whenever any county has. In the mtmnerand
method herein pointed out. adopted any county
government act. and the same shall have been
approved by the Legislature as aforesaid, the
directions of sections four and five of this
article providing for the uniformity of a
system of county governments throughout the
State and likewise providing for the election
and appointment of officers, and the regula-
tion of their compensation, shall not apply.
Said county government act 6hnll. as to any of
: the matters herelnabove provided for and de-
clared by Fuch county government act. not be
subject to any law or amendment enacted by
the Legislature, except by amendment first
submitted to the electors and ratified In the
manner herelnabove set forth.
AMENDMENT NVMBER THREE.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
44.)
A resolution proposing to the people of the
State of California an amendment to the Con-
stitution of the State, by adding a new section,
to be known and designated as section five and
one-half, article six, thereby providing for the
organization of a court, to be known as the
Court of Claims. The said proposed new sec-
tion to read as follows:
Section SH. The Court of Claims shall con-
sist of any three Judges of the Superior Court,
who may be requested by the Governor to hold
court at the regular terms thereof. The Court
of Claims shall have exclusive Jurisdiction to
hear and determine all claims of every kind
and character against the State, under such
laws as may be passed by the Legislature, and
Its judgment thereon shall be final. The terms
of the Court of Claims shall be held as fol-
lows:
In the City of Los Angeles, commencing on
the second Monday of March;
In the City and County of San Francisco,
commencing on the second Monday in July,
and
In the City of Sacramento, commencing on
the second Monday of November of each year.
The Judges holding *uch term of court shall
receive no extra compensation therefor, but
shall receive their actual expenses, to be paid
out of the general fund of the State treasury.
The Legislature shall enact all laws necessary
to organize such court, to provide the pro-
cedure thereof and to carry out the provisions
of this section.
AMENDMENT NUMBER FOUR.
(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment
No. 37.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the.
State of California an amendment to the Con-
stitution of the State, amending article eleven,
by adding a new section thereto, to be known
as section number five and one-half, relating to
consolidated city and county governments. The
said proposed new section to read as follows:
Section &V - The provisions of sections four
and five of this article shall not, nor shall any
lesialatlon passed pursuant thereto, apply to
any consolidated city and county government,
now existing or hereafter formed, which shall
have become, or shall become, organized under
section seven, or secure a charter under section
eight of this article.
AMENDMENT NfMBER FIVE.
(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment
No. *56.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California, an amendment o-f section
fifteen and section sixteen of article five of the
Constitution of the State of California, by
which It is proposed to amend said Bectlons to
read as follows:
Section 15. A Lieutenant Governor shall be
elected at the same time and place and in the
same manner aa the Governor, and his term of
office and his qualifications shall be the same.
He shall be president of the Senate, but shall
inly have a casting vote therein.
Section 18. In case of the Impeachment of
the Governor, or h\u removal from office, death,
inability to discharge the powers and duties
of his office, resignation, or absence from the
State, the powers and duties of the office shall
devolve upon the Lieutenant Governor for the
residue of the term, or until the disability
Bhall cease. And should the Lieutenant Gover-
nor be Impeached, displaced, resign, die, or
become Incapable of performing the duties of
his office, or. be absent rrom the State, the
president pro tempore of the Senate shall act
as Governor until the vacancy In the office of
Governor shall be filled at the next general
election when members of the Legislature shall
be chosen, or until such disability of the Lien-
tenant Governor shall cease. In case of a va-
cancy in the office of Governor for any of tha
reasons above named, and neither the Lieu-
tenant Governor nor the president pro tempore
of the Senate shall succeed to the powers and
duties of Governor, then the powers and duties
of such office shall devolve upon the Speaker
of the Assembly, nntU the office of Governor
â¢ shall be filled at such general election.

(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment

Section Â«. The public school system shall In-

hiph schools, evening schools, normal schools,

versity of California.
A^MENDMENT NUMBER SEVEN.

A NEW BODY
IN POLITICS.
Will Reorganize Within
a Few Days.
THE INDUSTRIES ACTIVE
A HOT CAMPAIGN FOR PURE
FOODS AND GOOD ROADS.
Several Big Commercial Bodies Unite
to Stand for Legislation Ben
ficial to the
State.
"Within the next ten days or so the As
sociated industries will be reorganised
and put in thape for the coming cam
paign. This organization came into ex
istence In December, ;; v .'t>. and was com
posed of the Manufacturers* and ro
ducers' Association, Chamber of Com
merce, Merchants' Association, State
Board of Trade, California Minors' Asso
ciation and the Mechanics' Institute. The
object was to further all legislation bene
ficial to California. During the earlier
part of its existence its members were
instrumental in .sending- a line exhibit of
the products of the State to the Guate
malan Exposition, resulting in an In
crease In trade between that country and
California. It also had inserted In the
â¢ Code the clause compelling all State of
ficials and managers of State Institutions
to purchase supplies of California make
and production. The body took a decided
stand against a scheme of certain poli
ticians which would have thrown many
free men out of employment and given
the work to convicts. The scheme was to
have ail the rock on county roads broken
by the Folsom crusher. The efforts of
the officers of the Industries limited the
output of this convict work until only
â a few roads in the neighborhood of the
prison were built of this rock. They also
fclookod the game of the Prison Directors
In the latter's efforts to introduce the
manufacture of many articles in the
prisons, thereby interfering with free
labor. , , .
The proposition to reoganize the body
has already nut with favor and to-day
at i he meeting of the Manufacturers t nd
J-roducers" Association President Sbar
boro will bring up the matter, having had
the assurance of nearly all at the old
members that they would be ready to
tcke up the work again. The plans for
future action have been practically
evolved, and before very long the body
will appear in politics in such a manner
as to command the attention of the poli
ticians. Among the objects to be at
tained is that of having passed stricter
pure food laws than now exist in the .stat
utes, so that all boards of health will
have the power to deal with the evils
of adulterated and impure foods. It is
proposed to have the laws so iron-bound
' that no red tape will be required to stamp
out the evil. The members of the organi
zation will make an effort to have a good
sized pure food plank introduced in the
platforms of the two big political par
ties and every legislative candidate will
1. "sounded." before he is indorsed by
the organization. The members are look
ing especially to the Republican party for
its indorsement of the pure food proposi
tion because of the stand taken by the
leader of that body. President McKinley.
It will be remembered that the strict pure
food laws of the State of Ohio were the
creation of Mr. McKinley, who made one
of the hardest rights of his life in hav
ing them become laws. In the coming
viection if a candidate has not commit
ted himself to the cause of the protection
of the health of the public in the matter
of pure foods he may expect opposition
from^*the commercial ami mercantile
bodies composing the Associated Indus
tries.
Another purpose 1b to co-operate with
those who have the good roads movement
in hand. The Qood Roads League of this
State has become a very strong organi
zation, its branches being established in
nearly every county, and even town, in
California. A combination of these two
bodies will cut a big figure in the coming
campaign. At the present time J. A. Fil
cher of the State Board of Trade is the.
president and Julian Sonntag the treas
urer of the Associated Industrie;;. Before
anything can be done in the matter of re
organizing the industries an election will
have to be hold, but this can be easily
effected as soon as the movement is
started at the meeting of the manufac
turers to-day.

The Spectacle of Cannon, the Fat Man,
in Tights Proves Too Much for
" Wallace."
THERE was a dress rehearsal at
the Chutes yesterday, the prin
cipals in which were Wallace,
the fierce^ African lion, and
Henri Maurice Cannon, whose claim
to fame is based on the fact that he
weighs over 600 pounds. It has been
arranged that he shall give public
exhibitions in the lion's cage and
the affair yesterday was in the na-
ture of a try out for both the man
nnd the king of beasts.
Wallace was removed from his
cage in the menagerie to another
erected in the open for exhibition
purposes. He at once made a sur
vey of his temporary quartors and
then awaited developments. Cap
tain Cardono, who is in charge of
the animals, was the first to enter
the cage and soon had his charge
growling and snapping viciously.
Attorney J. T. Fleming, who was on
the ground, was then induced to en
ter and spent an unpleasant ten
minxitts while Wallace was put
through his paces.

FIGHT TO-NIGHT
A SCIENTIFIC BATTLE IS PROM
ISED.
Tom Tracey and Frank McConnell
in First-Class Shape for a
Hard Contest.
If Tom Tracey Is not on the retrograde
he should surprise some of Frank Mc-
Donnell's most ardent admirers this even-
Ing. '
Reports have stated that Tracey Is not
physically as able a mail to-day as he
was on the evening he met George Green
in Woodward's Pavilion. There may be
some truth in the whisperings that have
been wafted from the other side of the
big mountains concerning Tracey's ways
of living since his last appearance In this
i city, but it goes without suying that his
look's belie all reports of a kind that may
do the Eastern welter-weight an Injus
tice. Tracey, to all outward appearance,
is in first-class condition, and if all goes
well he should give an excellent account
of himself this evening. True, he is not
as rapid a fighter as McKeever, but what
he is lacking in speed he fully makes up
for in punching powers. It must be re
â¢ membered that when he fought Green
he had to give away chunks of weight,
as Green at that time would not fight any
man who was at all near his (Green 8)
fighting weight. '
Tracey was born in Australia, and has
been fighting since 1891. His . first battle
of any importance was against Dummy
Mace in Melbourne, which ended in a
draw. His hot engagement in January,
1593, with Billy Gallagher of Oakland in
the old Pacific Club, which was then
; managed by Billy Harrison. Is still fresh
in the minds of many sport-loving citi
zens. Gallagher had Tracey all but out,
| but the Australian proved his great
sameness, and struggling to his feet in
an almost bewildered state he put his
; right on Gallagher's jaw, and that turned
the tide of battle. Oakland Blfty went out
in the nineteenth round. In April, lslM.
Tracey met the black demon Walcott and
was knocked out In the sixteenth round,
i after a hard battle. It was on the even
ing of October 8 that he met and was de
! feated by George Green in ten rounds.
McConnell's record is very short. His
only batle of note was that in which he
defeated McKeever, not by a chance
, blow, as many people suppose, but by a
! display of excellent judgment, backed up
< by the good fighting qualities that are
possessed by men who attain champion
! ship distinction.
McDonnell is unquestionably a polished
boxer, and his fight with McKeever im
pressed, many good judges, who did not
hesitate to pronounce him a rising star
; in the sky. of pugilism. .
Tracey will need use very great caution
i when he tangles up with the local cham
i pion this evening, as at close range Mo
! Connell can handle himself to the very
best of advantage, and his shifts are well
1 worthy of closest attention. In the sec
ond round of his light with McXeever.
McConnell executed what is known in
fighting parlance as a double shift, which
took the clever McKeever entirely by sur
prise; and an unguarded jaw was a target
: which McConnell missed by scarcely an
Inch. The blow would have ended the
; battle had it landed. Â ,-, â
The betting strongjy favors McConnell.
i but the odds are false, as Tracey Is a far
! better man that local sports imagine.
; Those who have laid $10 to JS may have
cause to regret their hasty action. The
i game is certainly not an easy one for

A COWARDLY BLOW.
James Leahy of tne Kansas Regi
ment Punished for Striking
a Woman.
James Leahy, a member of Company
A, Kansas regiment, appeared before
| Judge Mogan yesterday to answer a
! charge of battery upon Mrs. Clotllde
j Hourcade, who owns a shooting gallery
: on Pine and Dupont streets.
The evidence showed that Leahy and
1 a comrade patronized the shooting gal
lery Wednesday night and refused to
pay. As they turned to leave Mrs. Ilour
caae seized Leahy's comrade by the arm
i and threatened to call the police. She
I blew a police whistle and a crowd made
! a rush for the gallery. During the ex
citement Leahy went behind Mrs. Hour
( cade and struck her a violent blow In the
: face, blackening one of her eyes.
Leahy did not deny striking the blow,
and the Judge told him his action was
that of a coward to strike a woman. He
sentenced him to pay a flno of $30, with
the alternative of thirty days in the Coun
ty Jail.

HE CALLS CHRISTIAN
ENDEAVORERS FRAUDS
AN ANONYMOUS WHITER BIT
TERLY DENOUNCES THEM. ,;
Says Neither the Society Nor Its
Members Will Pay a Bill If
It Can Be Avoided.
In a recent issue of Our Youth, a paper
published In the Interests of the Boys'
Home at 26 Laurel place by A. Hutchin
son, appeared an article severely arraign
ing the Christian Endeavor Society , as a
whole and its members as individuals.
The author of the article, who. care
fully conceals his Identity, attempted to
get up a Christian Endeavor, column in
Our Youth, in hopes of gaining subscribers
to that paper, but failed, as the corre
sponding secretaries of the unions neglect
ed to send him any items. He also had a
I somewhat bitter experience as one of the
owners of the Pacific Coast Endeavorer,
which suspended publication over a 'year
ago, because, he says, the subscribers
failed to pay their subscriptions. All these
things have combined to turn hie love
Into hate, and he openly accuses the \En
deavorers of being swindlers who use re
ligion as a cloak to hide their innate
selfishness and crookedness. In his arti
cle he says;
We have known a district Christian Endeavor
Union to escape paying for a special edition
after It had been issued and circulated on
the barest technicality. We have known a
branch committee of the great '97 convention
to object to a printer's bill after being given
the lowest 'possible rates until It was too late
for the bill to be audited, and the printer was
thus compelled to make an unwilling contribu
tion to the '87 fund of over $10.
We are not Burprtsed when a hard-bakeS
sinner does something dishonest or mean, but
when a society whose members not only prom
ise to "strive to do whatever Christ would
have them do." but proclaim that fact by
meana of a badge, la guilty of meanness, It
(fives those who sneer at Christianity far
greater occasion for their gibes than all the
well-worn falsehood â¢of the ungodly.
In saying these things we are inspired by no
feelings of bitterness. We have outlived the
disappointment we felt ' when obliged to sus
pend our paper because the Endeavorors failed
i to pay their debtÃ. The society Is a grand one,
its leaders sincere and Us work great, but if
God would allow his clionen people under
Joshua to suffer defeat because of one Achan
he is not going to hold the Endeavor army en
tirely guiltless when they pass unrebuked lit
tle acts of petty dishonesty or meannees that
tend to cause the > world to sneer at Christ and
his followers as no better than the children
of the evil one.
Prominent members of the society,
when seen, disclaimed all knowledge or
unpaid bills of the convention, and point
to the fact that there was a surplus of
$1500 after all bills were paid. Little or
nothing is known ,of Mr. Hutchinson by
them, but the few who do know him de
scribe him as "just the kind of man who
would write such an article." He dis
claims having written it, but says that
every word of it is true and that he is
willing to vouch for it and can back up
I his statements with ample proof.

HUNTING FOR
A LIBRARIAN
A Successor Wanted for
Arthur Jellison.
ONLY ONE NAME SUGGESTED
DIRECTORS WANT AN IDEAL
MAN FOR THE PLACE.
H. L. Ticknor Claims the Mechanics'
Library Is Deplorably
Behind the
* / Times.
Ever since the death of Arthur M. Jel
llson, the librarian of the Mechanics' In
stitute, it has been a matter of conjec
ture as to who will be appointed to the
responsible position as his successor.
Rumor has given birth to various theo
ries and has outlined a host of appli
cants, but these are denied by those di
rectly concerned in the management of
the institute.
H. L. Ticknor, chairman of the em
ployes committee, averred yesterday that
no one had been mentioned for the posi
tion and that the board of directors had
not decided as yet as to who was the
most eligible man to succeed Jellison.
He maintained that it was the univer
sal decision of the members of the board
to get the best man obtainable to ful
fill the duties of the position.
When asked as to whether Graves, the
acting librarian and Jellison's former as
sistant, would be ujiowed to take charge
of the institute as permanent librarian,
he said that Graves was a good man and
had done much in the interests of the
library, but inferred and clearly indicated
that he was of the impression that he
was not the best man that the directors
were vainly seeking for.
"We want a librarian," said Mr. Tick
nor, "that Is a man of letters, capable of
guiding the public in the selection of lit
erature. A man holding such a position
should be well educated and conversant
with the different and various books of
the library and capable of advising and
instructing subscribers in the choice of
books. He should lend, by his qualifica
tions, a certain prestige to the Institute
which would undoubtedly encourage the
public and increase the patronage of the
library.
"Who will be the librarian," he con
tinued, "depends solely on the man best
capable of conforming to the ideal libra
rian as outlined by the directors.
"We are behind the times and, knowing
this, we must do our best to keep abreast
of them by selecting careful, capable and
efficient officers to control and build the
library to what it should be. We have
been in a state of inertia for years and
It is now about time that we should en
deavor to take a step forward. We are
like the merchants of this cityâ living
buck in the days of '49.
"In the selection of an officer to fill
the vacancy occasioned by the death of
Mr. Jellison we have determined to make
this our initial effort in the furtherance
of the work contemplated. There has
been no one as yet found who has suited
us, and we propose to wait until the right
and the best man steps forward to assume
the responsibility and the duties involved
in the acceptance of the position.
"The rumor that an Kastern man of
education and extensive experience has j
been decided upon for the vacancy Is lin- j
true. There has been absolutely no one :
suggested and absolutely not the slightest |
indication that there will be in the near i
future."
F. B. Graves, in speaking of his chances
of becoming the permanent librarian of
the institute, stated that, although he
has no definite knowledge as to the prob
able action of the board in his favor,
still he thinks that his name should meet
with considerable consideration, as he
has been connected with the library for
nearly fourteen years, lie oialms that
all the directors are not favorably dis
posed toward him in the matter of the
appointment, but the majority are, which
fact, he hopes, will be proven at the elt?c
tion which will be held in about sixty
days. In all probability the names of sev
eral applicants will be presented to the
board for consideration at the next regu
lar semi-monthly meeting.
BONDED WAREHOUSES
WILL GET THE TEA
SHIPPEKS PBEPARING A PRO
TEST TO WASHINGTON.
They Contend That the New Ruling
Imposes a Burden on Consignees
Without Benefit to the
Government.
Importers of tea are very much exer
; clsed over a ruling' of the Secretary of
I the Treasury to the effect that all shlp
: merits of the fragrant herb must be
| placed In bonded warehouse until the tea
| has been passed upon by the Tea Ex
aminer and the duty of 10 cents per
pound paid upon it. Heretofore they
; have been In the habit of storing the tea
i In their own warehouses, thus saving: the
i bonded warehouse charges, but under
the new ruling they will be put to an
expense which they consider unneces-
sary.
Hence the shippers and consignees liavo
prepared a strong: protest, which they
will forward to-day by tolesraph to
Washington, asking for the rescinding of
the rule. They contend that It imposes
a burden additional to the tax, and that
the Interests of the Government would
be just as well subserved by allowing the
protestants to store the tea In their own
warehouses, as heretofore.
