VINEYABDIST DEAD.
R. W. Lemme, Superintendent of the
California Wine Cellars,
Passes Away.
Randolph W. Lemme, one of the best
known vineyardists and wine makers in
the State, died at his home near St. He
lena last Wedneseday evening after a
short illness. His death was a surprise
to every one who knew him, as he seem
ingly enjoyed the best of health.
As superintendent of the California
Wine Corporation's cellars of this city he
devoted his time and ability to advancing
the interests of the wine producers of the
State as distinguished from wine deal
ers While engaged in his undertaking
ho had a slight attack of what he thought
was Indigestion, but which proved more
serious. His heart became Involved and
its accelerated motion baffled the efforts
of his physician and after two weeks Ill
ness he was relieved by death.
The deceased was born in this city and
received his education in the public
schools of this State. In ISS3 he married
Miss Alice McPike of St. Helena, and he
leaves a widow and three children.
The family services will be held at the
family residence near Crane Station,
NÂ»i pa County, at 1:30 o'clock to-day. The
remains will be brought to the Odd Fel
lows' Cemeterey in this city on Saturday
morning and will be cremated at noon.

REPUDIATES THE
"ONE HUNDRED."
R. P. Doolan, the Member
From the Thlrty-Finst, De
clines to Name the
Delegates.
One member of the Democratic commit
tee of 100 has shown marked disapproval
of the rule established by that body,
which seeks to deprive the Democrats of
San Francisco of the right to name dele
gates to the State convention.
R. P. Doolan went before tho meeting
of the Thirty-first Assembly District
Democratic Club last night and informed
his fellow members that as a member of
the committee of 100 he proposed to break
a rule of that eommtttee, or words to that
effect, by relinquishing 1 to tho Democratic
club of the district the right to choose
ten of the eleven delegates to the State
convention, to which the district is en
titled. Whereupon Mr. Doolan was ap
plauded. He proposed, however, to keep
the list within the membership of the
club, and announced his intention of se
lecting his appointees from the list direct
ly it was presented to him, so that if
there should be any objection made it
could be made before the committee of
100 got a chance at it. . He proposed to
let nls club actually name the dele
gates.
Mr. Doolan was ngain applauded. And
then he was interrupted by an outsider,
who was denounced, and who declared
that the member of the com
mittee of Democratic bosses was
putting up a Job. So Mr. Doolan
decided that he would take time
to think over the names nnd consult the
persons named. Sixteen names were pre
sented, from which ten will be selected as
the club's delegates, and Mr. Doolan him
self will name the eleventh. The sixteen
are those:
Dr. Robert J. Lowroy, M. Cella, John
Reed, M. J. Pilva, Thomas Clancy, C. J.
Gallagher. Major Enright. John Mc-
Geary. Thomas Hlckey. T. E. Tracy, D.
J. Qulnlan, Jeremiah J. Sheahan, Patrick
Savage, John McDonald, J. J. Joell,
James McElearney.
The Tommy Bree Banjo and Mandolin
Club and Messrs. O'Neil, Hahn, Kelley,
Peaaley and Schwartz served the club
with music in preparation for a speech
by Judge Robert Ferral.
Thomas Hickey and Mr. Clnrk also ad
dressed tho meeting and Timothy E.
Tracy marie a little speech declining to
bo sent to Sacramento as a delegate, pro
vided ho should be appointed from the
list chosen. And the meeting broke up
harmoniously.

HARNEY WING WILL
NOMINATE DELEGATES
District Clubs Will Begin Choos-
ing Representatives to the
Convention To-Night.
An air of quietness pervaded the head
quarters of the Harney Democracy in the
Baldwin Hotel last evening. The political
situation was discussed by a few dozen
followers, seated about the room. The en
rollment, which began on Wednesday
evening, was still in progress, and sev
eral thousand additional names have
horn added to the list. To-night the va
rious district clubs will begin nominating
their delegates to the Sacramento conven
tion, and by Monday evening it is thought
the entire list of those who will Journey
to the capital will be completed.
MAKING PLANS FOR
THE CAMPAIGN
The Dewey Central Republican
Club Will Soon Establish
Headquarters.
Plans for the coming State and munici
pal campaigns were discussed at a meet
ing of the executive committee of the
Dewey Central Republican Club last
evening in the Columbian building. The
committee appointed at a previous meet
ing to secure headquarters for the club
reported that several central locations
can be secured at any. time, and at the
next meeting a report in favor of one of
them will be presented.
Branch clubs will be organized through
out the city and State.
It Is the Intention of the committee on
speakers to invite Judge Spencer of ho
noma County to deliver an address at the
first Republican mass meeting held in the
city. Congressman Barham will also be
invited to speak from the same plat
form.
Resolutions indorsing the patriotic, in
telligent and firm administration of Presi
dent McKinley were passed and spread
upon the minutes. Chairman Henry
Mayer announced that the executive com
mittee will meet again next Monday
night to continue preparations for a vig
orous fight against the Demo-Populist
combination at the approaching election.
The Bear Club.
The Bear Republican Club held a large
ly attended meeting in its new head
quarters in B'nal B'rith Hall, with T. W.
Collins presiding, last night. The secre
tary read letters from thirty-eight mem
bers who took part in the last campaign
in reply to postals sent out, stating they
desired to retain their membership In the
club. Merton C. Allen, School Director E.
L. Head, W. H. Schooler, A. B. Truman
and others made remarks for good of the
organization. It was resolved to meet
weekly hereafter instead of monthly,
Thursday evening being the night select
ed. The ciub has been in existence Â«ince
18S1 and has participated in every cam
paign since then for the success of the
Republican party.
Railroad Men Enthusiastic.
The United Railroad Republican Club
held a rousing meeting at Washington
Hall, 35 Eddy street, last night. The
names of 170 applicants were added to
the roll, making a total membership of
590. This club's membership is composed
entirely of employes of the various street
railroads of this city. Motorman G. H.
Davis addressed the meeting with a very
able speech. Songs followed by R. B. Ba
ker, William Kolso, J. S. "W'ainant, Ralph
Marsh and "William Ramsey, and a rec
itation by Frank Miller. The Market
street Railway band furnished music for
the meeting.

Dixon's New Hat Store.
The many friends of Mr. S. W. Dix
on, the popular hatter, will be pleased
to learn that he haf severed his con
nection with the Emporium and has
opened an elegant new hat store at 7C7
Market street, opposite the Phelan
building.
Always alive to the demands of the
public in his line, Mr. Dlxon offers a
superior quality of hats at popular
prices. He has a complete stock of the
most seasonable goods direct from the
best Eastern manufacturers. Mr. Dix
on, contrary to the prevailing custom,
is ready to take a reasonable profit on
a volume of sales, instead of demand
ing while goods are new a price for
Â«tvle and reputation, that as soon as
shop worn dealers are accustomed to
sell for half price. A specialty is made
of $1 $2 and $3 hats. The reputation
for reliability in his business methods
enjoyed in the past by Mr. Dixon is an
assurance of further success in his new
departure.
Two Hospital Ships Obtained.
Dr Carvoslur of the United States
army informed the State Red Cross So
ciety yesterday that the transports Scan
dla and Arizona would be used as hospital
ships on their arrival at Manila. The Red
Cross Society will be permitted to supply
nurses and luxuries for the sick and
wounded.
Mrs W B. Harrington, president of the
State Red Cross Society, telegraphed to
Washington yesterday to obtain permis
sion for her society to erect a house wi ta
in the Presidio grounds to be devoted to
a home for convalescents. If the request
is granted a building will be immediately
erected as no house suitable for the pur-
Do^e named can be had within the res
ervation or close by. The Government
will furnish medicines and a man to take
care of the establishment, whilo the Red
Cnws will furnish the house completely
and supply the necessary nurses.

Notwithstanding the somewhat an
noying publicity of the Hollander-

and the accidental fall received while

the consequences of which have

leine Bouton, late leading lady of the

Miss Bouton is soon to be married.
She has caught a German Baron. His
name is Guido yon Nimptsch.
Miss Bouton, who is at the McNutt
Hospital, made the happy confession
last evening mid tears and smiles. It
was drawn from her by means of a
dispatch from New York, which was
shown her. The telegram stated that
a sensation had been created in New
York by the announcement that she
had been married to Baron yon
Nimptsch more than a year ago, and
that the fact had been carefully con
cealed all this time, as the Baron's

their first meeting, as told in the tele

would not be inappropriate in one of
SISTER CLAY DISPUTES MADELEINE.
NEW YORK, Aug. -s.â Miss Clay St. John, a sister of Medeleine Bou
ton, in an interview yesterday said:
"My sister has been Baroness yon Nimptsch for over a year and a
half. She and the Baron were married in New York In December of
1896. I was told by Madeleine to keep the news a secret. The Baron did
not care to have his relatives in Germany know of the marriage for fear
he might be disinherited for marrying an actress."
the plays in which Miss Bouton has
made so many successes:
"It came about through a rainy day
on Broadway. Miss Bouton's maid was
hurrying past the Hoffman House to
reach the elevated station, when her
foot slipped. There was a wild effort
to balance herself, she fell, and then
gave a cry of pain. All this happened
just as Baron yon Nimptsch was being
driven down Broadway in his carriage
on his way to his office in the Empire
building. He saw that she was seri
ously hurt, and taking her in his arms
he carried her to a near-by drug store.
There a doctor was summoned and it
was found that the maid's ankle was
badly sprained. There was no possi
bility of her walking, ani for her to
try to get home alone was out of the
question. The gallant Baron placed his
carriage at her service and accompa
nied her to the flat where Miss Bouton
was living at the time. As the rescuer
of her maid the Baron was introduced
to Miss Bouton, and was frequently
seen to call at the flat afterward, and
finally they were married."
Miss Bouton read this dispatch with
the deepest interest. Then drawing a
long sigh she burst into tears and ex
claimed, "Why can't they let me
alone!" The next moment she broke
into smiles and laughter.
"That story is all rot! It's a fairy
story!" said she. The rapid change
from tears to Laughter was quite an
emotional scene, and was much enjoyed
by the audience: "Then it is not true
that you're a Baroness?"
"Not yet, but I hope soon to be," she
replied. "I shall be married to Baron
Guido yon Nimptsch a^ soon as I re
cover from my illness. I suppose I
might as well tell all about it now, in
order to correct that silly New York
story.
"I became acquainted with the Ba
ron eighteen months ago. My maid
didn't cut any figure in that romance..
That sprained ankle of hers was not
one of the properties in the play. I
first met the Baron at a dinner party
at the residence of a well-known fam
ily â most charming people â who were
among my most intimate friends. He
became very attentive to me, and that
acquaintance ripened into love. He
asked me to be his wife; I made him
happy by giving him an affirmative
answer. That was one. year ago.
Since that time we have been most de
voted to each other.
"Just before coming to San Francisco
I had the choice of going to Europe on
a wedding trip or playing a season's
engagement with the Frawley com-
pany. Being wrapped up in my pro
fession, and having secured a rich and
elegant wardrobe, my woman's pride
naturally led me to appear upon the
stage for one more season, the under
standing being that we should then be
married.
"I bade the Baron good-by and came
west, while he went over to Europe on
important business. You know the rest.
Here lamon a sick bed. They are
building me up in order to prepare me
for a surgical operation which I must
undergo in a few days. God only
knows whether I will come out of it."
"Does the Baron know of your mis
fortune?" she was asked.
"Oh, yes! I cabled him yesterday,
and he Ju-s answered that he will come
to San Francisco at once. I have re
ceived letters Jrom him in every Eu
ropean mail. Here's his last letter in
which he says he intended to sail for
New York on August 15, and might be
obliged to go to Chicago, and possibly
to San Francisco."
"I just think the Baron is the only
pebble on the beach. Please excuse
the expression, as it conveys my feel
ing for him. I am very proud of him.
He comes from a distinguished family
âthe Hatzfeldt fa-nily. He is a cousin
of that Prince Hatzfeldt who married
Miss Huntington of this city. .
By reference to the Almanach de
Gotha it is learned that Baron Guido
yon Nimptsch is the son of the Coun
tess Francoise Hatzfeldt and Paul yon
Nimptsch of Jaeschkowitz. Tschirne
and Janowltz in Silesia, who were mar
ried in 1849. He is about 47 years of
